<span id="page-44-1"></span>**Lemma 4.1.**  $\Pi_{\mathsf{ccs}}$  is a reduction of knowledge from  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{cmccs}}$  to  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{evalccs}}$  for any bound  $B \in \mathbb{N}$ . *Proof.* We defer the proof to Appendix B.1.

# <span id="page-44-0"></span>**4.2.2** Decomposition: The reduction from $(\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^B)^2$ to $(\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^b)^{2k}$

Next, we describe the decomposition step that splits the witnesses and reduces the norms. By Theorem 2.2, it suffices to construct a protocol  $\Pi^*_{\mathsf{ccsdec}}$  that reduces  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{evalccs}}$  to  $(\mathcal{R}^b_{\mathsf{evalccs}})^k$ , and the reduction of knowledge from  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{evalccs}} \times \mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{evalccs}}$  to  $(\mathcal{R}^b_{\mathsf{evalccs}})^{2k}$  is  $\Pi_{\mathsf{ccsdec}} := \Pi^*_{\mathsf{ccsdec}} \times \Pi^*_{\mathsf{ccsdec}}$  that runs two instances of  $\Pi^*_{\mathsf{ccsdec}}$  in parallel.

More generally, we construct a reduction of knowledge from a relation  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{ccshom}}$  to  $(\mathcal{R}^b_{\mathsf{ccshom}})^k$ . Here  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{ccshom}}$  is a generalization of  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{evalccs}}$  (Definition 35) where we generalize Ajtai commitments and gadget matrix multiplications to arbitrary  $\mathcal{R}_q$ -module homomorphisms. Let  $\mathcal{U} := \mathcal{R}^t_q$  for some  $t \in \mathbb{N}$ . Let  $\overline{M} \in \mathcal{U}^{(n+n_{\mathsf{in}}) \times m}$  denote a matrix over module  $\mathcal{U}$ . Let  $\mathcal{L} : \mathcal{R}^m_q \to \mathcal{Y}$  and  $\mathcal{L}_w : \mathcal{R}^m_q \to \mathcal{R}^{n+n_{\mathsf{in}}}_q$  denote some  $\mathcal{R}_q$ -module homomorphisms. We define the relation  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{ccshom}}$  as

<span id="page-44-4"></span>
$$\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{ccshom}}^{B} := \left\{ \begin{array}{l} \mathsf{pp} := (\mathcal{L}, \mathcal{L}_{w}, \overline{M}), \\ \mathsf{x} := (\vec{\mathbf{r}} \in \mathcal{R}_{q}^{\log m}, \hat{\mathbf{v}} \in \mathcal{R}_{q}, \mathbf{u} \in \mathcal{U}, y \in \mathcal{Y}, \mathsf{x}_{w} \in \mathcal{R}_{q}^{n_{\mathsf{in}}}); \\ \mathsf{w} := (\vec{\mathbf{f}} \in \mathcal{R}_{q}^{m}, \vec{\mathbf{w}} \in \mathcal{R}_{q}^{n}) \text{ s.t.} \\ (\mathcal{L}, (\vec{\mathbf{r}}, \hat{\mathbf{v}}, y); \vec{\mathbf{f}}) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{hom}}^{B} \land \\ (\mathbf{z} = \mathcal{L}_{w}(\vec{\mathbf{f}})) \land (\mathbf{u} = \langle \overline{M} \times \mathsf{tensor}(\vec{\mathbf{r}}), \mathbf{z} \rangle) \end{array} \right\}, \tag{36}$$

where  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{hom}}^B$  is defined in Eq. (10),  $\mathbf{z} := (\mathbf{z}_w || \vec{\mathbf{w}}) \in \mathcal{R}_q^{n_{\mathsf{in}} + n}$  and

<span id="page-44-5"></span>
$$tensor(\vec{\mathbf{r}}) := \bigotimes_{i=1}^{\log m} (\vec{\mathbf{r}}_i, 1 - \vec{\mathbf{r}}_i) \in \mathcal{R}_q^m$$
(37)

is the tensor product of  $\{(\vec{\mathbf{r}}_i, 1 - \vec{\mathbf{r}}_i)\}_{i=1}^{\log m}$ .

<span id="page-44-3"></span>**Remark 4.4.**  $\mathcal{R}_{\text{evalccs}}^{B}$  is a special case of  $\mathcal{R}_{\text{ccshom}}^{B}$  where  $\mathcal{R}_{\text{hom}}^{B} := \mathcal{R}_{\text{eval}}^{B}$ ;  $\mathcal{L}_{w}(\vec{\mathbf{f}}) := \mathbf{G}\vec{\mathbf{f}}$  (where  $\mathbf{G}$  is the gadget matrix);  $\mathcal{U} := \mathcal{R}_{q}^{t}$ ;  $\mathbf{z}_{w} := (\mathbf{z}_{\text{ccs}}, \mathbf{h})$ ; and  $\overline{M} := (M_{1}, \ldots, M_{t})$ , i.e., each entry  $(\mathbf{e}_{1}, \ldots, \mathbf{e}_{t}) \in \mathcal{U}$  of  $\overline{M}$  maps to the entries  $(\mathbf{e}_{i})_{i=1}^{t}$  in matrices  $M_{1}, \ldots, M_{t}$  respectively.

We describe the protocol  $\Pi^*_{\mathsf{ccsdec}}$  in Figure 6. The differences from  $\Pi^*_{\mathsf{dec}}$  (Figure 2) are highlighted in red , which are for computing the **u**-values and the CCS instances.

<span id="page-44-2"></span>**Lemma 4.2.** Fix  $\mathcal{R}_q \cong \mathbb{Z}_q^d$ . For any B < q/2 and any b, k such that  $b^k = B$ ,  $\Pi_{\mathsf{ccsdec}}^*$  is a reduction of knowledge from  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{ccshom}}^B$  to  $(\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{ccshom}}^b)^k$ .

*Proof.* The proof is similar to that for Lemma 3.3. We defer the proof to Appendix B.2.

```
Input: \mathbf{x} := (\vec{\mathbf{r}}, \hat{\mathbf{v}}, y, \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{x}_{w}) and \mathbf{w} := (\vec{\mathbf{f}}, \vec{\mathbf{w}})

Output: [\mathbf{x}_{i} = (\vec{\mathbf{r}}, \hat{\mathbf{v}}_{i}, y_{i}, \mathbf{u}_{i}, \mathbf{x}_{w,i}), \mathbf{w}_{i} = (\vec{\mathbf{f}}_{i}, \vec{\mathbf{w}}_{i})]_{i=0}^{k-1}

The protocol \langle \mathsf{P}(\mathsf{pp}, \mathbf{x}; \mathbf{w}), \mathsf{V}(\mathsf{pp}, \mathbf{x}) \rangle:

1. \mathsf{P} computes (\vec{\mathbf{f}}_{0}, \dots, \vec{\mathbf{f}}_{k-1}) \leftarrow \mathsf{split}_{b,k}(\vec{\mathbf{f}}) (Eq. (11)).

2. \mathsf{P} \rightarrow \mathsf{V} : \mathsf{P} sends \mathsf{V} the values [y_{i}, \hat{\mathbf{v}}_{i}, \mathbf{u}_{i}, \mathbf{x}_{w,i}]_{i=0}^{k-1} where

y_{i} := \mathcal{L}(\vec{\mathbf{f}}_{i}), \qquad \hat{\mathbf{v}}_{i} := \mathsf{mle}\left[\hat{\mathbf{f}}_{i}\right](\vec{\mathbf{r}}),
\mathbf{u}_{i} := \left\langle \overline{M} \times \mathsf{tensor}(\vec{\mathbf{r}}), \mathcal{L}_{w}(\vec{\mathbf{f}}_{i}) \right\rangle, \qquad \mathbf{x}_{w,i} := \mathcal{L}_{w}(\vec{\mathbf{f}}_{i})[1, n_{\mathsf{in}}]

3. \mathsf{V} checks that \sum_{i=0}^{k-1} b^{i} \cdot [y_{i}, \hat{\mathbf{v}}_{i}, \mathbf{u}_{i}, \mathbf{x}_{w,i}] \stackrel{?}{=} [y, \hat{\mathbf{v}}, \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{x}_{w}].

4. \mathsf{V} outputs [\mathbf{x}_{i} = (\vec{\mathbf{r}}, \hat{\mathbf{v}}_{i}, y_{i}, \mathbf{u}_{i}, \mathbf{x}_{w,i})]_{i=0}^{k-1}. \mathsf{P} outputs [\mathbf{w}_{i} = (\vec{\mathbf{f}}_{i}, \mathcal{L}_{w}(\vec{\mathbf{f}}_{i}))]_{i=0}^{k-1}.
```

<span id="page-45-2"></span>Figure 6: The protocol  $\Pi_{\text{ccsdec}}^*$  that reduces  $\mathcal{R}_{\text{ccshom}}^B$  to  $(\mathcal{R}_{\text{ccshom}}^b)^k$ .

# <span id="page-45-0"></span>4.2.3 Folding: The reduction from $(\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^b)^{2k}$ to $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^B$

Finally, we describe the core protocol  $\Pi_{\sf ccsfold}$  that folds 2k instance-witness pairs of  $\mathcal{R}^b_{\sf evalccs}$  into a single instance-witness pair in  $\mathcal{R}_{\sf acc} := \mathcal{R}^B_{\sf evalccs}$ .

Similar to the treatment in Section 3.2.3, in the following, we assume that the homomorphism  $\mathcal{L}$  in the public parameter is 2B-relaxed binding (Section 2.3) for challenge space  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$ . For example,  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{evalccs}}$  is a special case of  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{ccshom}}$  by Remark 4.4 and the homomorphism  $\mathcal{L}(\vec{\mathbf{f}}) := \mathbf{A}\vec{\mathbf{f}}$  is 2B-relaxed binding given the hardness of  $\mathsf{MSIS}_{\kappa,m,8TB}^{\infty,q}$  where  $T = \|\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}\|_{\mathsf{op}}$ .

We describe the protocol  $\Pi_{\mathsf{ccsfold}}$  in Figure 7, which reduces from  $(\mathcal{R}^b_{\mathsf{ccshom}})^{2k}$  to  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{ccshom}}$ . The approach is similar to that in Section 3.2.3, where we fold the witnesses using small random scalars from a strong sampling set, and run sum-check to enable extractions of small-norm witnesses. For brevity, we assume that  $\mathcal{U} := \mathcal{R}_q$ , hence t = 1 and the matrix  $\overline{M} = M_1 \in \mathcal{R}_q^{(n+n_{\mathsf{in}}) \times m}$ . The protocol naturally extends to the case when  $\mathcal{U} := \mathcal{R}_q^t$  for t > 1: we set  $\{\mathbf{u}_i, \eta_i\}$  to be elements over  $\mathcal{R}_q^t$ , sample challenges  $\{\zeta_i\}$  over  $\mathcal{C}^t$  and replace the multiplications between  $\zeta_i$  and  $\mathbf{u}_i$  (and  $\eta_i$ ) with inner product operations.

<span id="page-45-1"></span>**Theorem 4.2.** Let  $\mathcal{R}_q \cong \mathbb{Z}_q^d$ . Let  $\mathcal{C}$ ,  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$  be strong sampling sets where  $1/|\mathcal{C}|$ ,  $1/|\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  and  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$  has expansion factor at most c (Definition 6). Let  $\mathsf{pp} := (m, n, n_{\mathsf{in}}, B, \mathcal{L}, \mathcal{L}_w)$  be the public parameter where the homomorphism  $\mathcal{L}$  is 2B-relaxed binding (Section 2.3) for challenge space  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$ . For any b, k such that 2kc(b-1) < B,  $\Pi_{\mathsf{ccsfold}}$  is a reduction of knowledge from  $(\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{ccshom}}^b)^{2k}$  to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{ccshom}}^B$ .

*Proof.* The proof is similar to that for Theorem 3.2. We defer the proof to Appendix B.3.

**Parameters:**  $c \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $\mathcal{C} := \mathbb{Z}_q \subseteq \mathcal{R}_q$  and a strong sampling set  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$  with  $\|\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}\|_{\mathsf{op}} \leq c$ 

Input:  $\mathbf{x} := [\mathbf{x}_i := (\vec{\mathbf{r}}_i, \hat{\mathbf{v}}_i, y_i, \mathbf{u}_i, \mathbf{x}_{w,i})]_{i=1}^{2k}$  and  $\mathbf{w} := [\mathbf{w}_i := (\vec{\mathbf{f}}_i, \vec{\mathbf{w}}_i)]_{i=1}^{2k}$ 

Output:  $\aleph_o := (\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o, \hat{\mathbf{v}}_o, y_o, \mathbf{u}_o, \mathbf{v}_{w,o}), \ \aleph_o := (\vec{\mathbf{f}}_o, \mathbf{\vec{w}}_o)$ 

The protocol  $\langle P(pp, x; w), V(pp, x) \rangle$ :

- 1.  $V \to P : V \text{ sends } P \left[\alpha_i, \mu_i, \zeta_i\right]_{i=1}^{2k} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} (\mathcal{C} \times \mathcal{C} \times \mathcal{C})^{2k} \text{ and } \vec{\beta} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{C}^{\log m}.$
- 2.  $V \leftrightarrow P : P$  and V run a sum-check protocol for the claim

<span id="page-46-2"></span>
$$\sum_{\vec{\mathbf{b}} \in \{0,1\}^{\log m}} g(\vec{\mathbf{b}}) = \sum_{i=1}^{2k} (\alpha_i \hat{\mathbf{v}}_i + \zeta_i \mathbf{u}_i).$$

Here the polynomial  $g(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) \in \mathcal{R}_q^{\leq 2b}[X_1, \dots, X_{\log m}]$  is defined as

$$g(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) := \sum_{i=1}^{2k} \left[ \alpha_i g_{1,i}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) + \mu_i g_{2,i}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) + \zeta_i g_{3,i}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) \right], \tag{38}$$

where for all  $i \in [2k]$ ,

$$g_{1,i}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) := eq(\vec{\mathbf{r}}_i, \vec{\mathbf{x}}) \cdot \mathsf{mle}\left[\hat{\mathbf{f}}_i\right](\vec{\mathbf{x}}) \,, \quad g_{2,i}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) := eq(\vec{\beta}, \vec{\mathbf{x}}) \cdot \prod_{j=-(b-1)}^{b-1} \left(\mathsf{mle}\left[\hat{\mathbf{f}}_i\right](\vec{\mathbf{x}}) - j\right) \,,$$

$$g_{3,i}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) \coloneqq eq(\vec{\mathbf{r}}_i, \vec{\mathbf{x}}) \cdot \left( \sum_{\vec{\mathbf{b}} \in \{0,1\}^{\log(n+n_{\mathrm{in}})}} \mathsf{mle}\left[M_1\right](\vec{\mathbf{x}}, \vec{\mathbf{b}}) \cdot \mathsf{mle}\left[\mathbf{z}_i\right](\vec{\mathbf{b}}) \right) \, .$$

Here  $\mathbf{z}_i := (\mathbf{z}_{w,i} || \vec{\mathbf{w}}_i)$  for all  $i \in [2k]$ . The protocol reduces to check the evaluation claim  $g(\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o) \stackrel{?}{=} s$  where  $\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{C}^{\log m}$  is the sum-check challenge sampled by V.

3.  $P \to V : P \text{ sends } V \text{ values } \left[\theta_i := \mathsf{mle}\left[\hat{\mathbf{f}}_i\right](\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o), \frac{\eta_i}{i}\right]_{i=1}^{2k}$ , where for all  $i \in [2k]$ ,

$$\eta_i := \sum_{\vec{\mathbf{b}} \in \{0,1\}^{\log{(n+n_{\mathrm{in}})}}} \mathsf{mle}\left[M_1\right](\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o,\vec{\mathbf{b}}) \cdot \mathsf{mle}\left[\mathbf{z}_i\right](\vec{\mathbf{b}}) \,.$$

<span id="page-46-1"></span>4. V computes  $[\mathbf{e}_i := eq(\vec{\mathbf{r}}_i, \vec{\mathbf{r}}_o)]_{i=1}^{2k}$  and  $\mathbf{e}^* := eq(\vec{\beta}, \vec{\mathbf{r}}_o)$  and checks that

$$s \stackrel{?}{=} \sum_{i=1}^{2k} \left[ \alpha_i \mathbf{e}_i \theta_i + \mu_i \mathbf{e}^* \cdot \prod_{j=1-b}^{b-1} (\theta_i - j) + \zeta_i \mathbf{e}_i \eta_i \right].$$

- 5.  $V \to P : V$  sends P random challenges  $[\rho_i]_{i=1}^{2k} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{C}^{2k}_{\mathsf{small}}$ .
- 6. V output  $\mathbb{X}_o := (\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o, \hat{\mathbf{v}}_o, y_o, \mathbf{u}_o, \mathbb{X}_{w,o})$  where  $\mathsf{NTT}(\hat{\mathbf{v}}_o) = \sum_{i=1}^{2k} \mathsf{RotSum}(\rho_i, \mathsf{NTT}(\theta_i))$  and  $[y_o, \mathbf{u}_o, \mathbf{x}_{w,o}] := \sum_{i=1}^{2k} \rho_i \cdot [y_i, \eta_i, \mathbf{x}_{w,i}].$ 7. P outputs  $\vec{\mathbf{f}}_o = \sum_{i=1}^{2k} \rho_i \cdot \vec{\mathbf{f}}_i$  and  $\vec{\mathbf{w}}_o := \mathcal{L}_w(\vec{\mathbf{f}}_o)[n_{\mathsf{in}} + 1, n_{\mathsf{in}} + n]$ .
- <span id="page-46-0"></span>

Figure 7: The protocol  $\Pi_{\mathsf{ccsfold}}$  that reduces  $(\mathcal{R}^b_{\mathsf{ccshom}})^{2k}$  to  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{ccshom}}$ . The  $\mathcal{R}_q$ -module  $\mathcal{U}$  is set to  $\mathcal{U} := \mathcal{R}_q$  for brevity.

### <span id="page-47-0"></span>4.3 An Optimized Folding Scheme for CCS

Recall that the folding scheme in Section 4.2 requires two sequential sumcheck executions. The first sumcheck is in the linearization protocol  $\Pi_{ccs}$  (Figure 5) that reduces  $\mathcal{R}^B_{evalccs}$ ; the second sumcheck is in the folding protocol  $\Pi_{ccsfold}$  (Figure 7) that reduces  $(\mathcal{R}^b_{evalccs})^{2k}$  to  $\mathcal{R}^B_{evalccs}$ . Note that a decomposition protocol (Figure 6) for witness norm deduction is executed in the middle, thus it is unclear how to batch the two sumchecks into one given that the witnesses of the two sumchecks are quite different.

Fortunately, with a simple trick, we build a folding scheme for CCS that executes sumcheck only once. If the CCS gate degree deg and the range parameter b are set such that deg  $\approx 2b$ , both the prover time and verifier complexity can be improved by a factor of two. Moreover, the prover saves the computation of an Ajtai commitment to the witness.

The core observation is that we can decompose the witness before running the linearization protocol. Recall that in the committed CCS relation  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{cmccs}}$  in Definition 4.2, the instance consists of a CCS public input  $\mathbb{Z}_{\mathsf{w}}$  and a commitment cm, and the witness is a pair of vectors  $(\vec{\mathbf{f}}, \vec{\mathbf{w}})$  such that

- $(\mathbf{x}_{\mathsf{ccs}}, \vec{\mathbf{w}})$  is in the CCS relation,
- $\vec{\mathbf{f}}$  is an opening of cm with norm less than B, and
- $\vec{\mathbf{w}} = \mathbf{G} \times \vec{\mathbf{f}}$  where  $\mathbf{G}$  is the gadget matrix.

Instead of transforming the CCS relation to  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{cmccs}}$ , we consider a variant of  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{cmccs}}$  called splitted committed CCS relations. Set parameter  $b, k \in \mathbb{N}$  such that  $b^k = B$ . The instance now consists of k vectors  $[\mathbf{z}_{\mathsf{w},i}]_{i=1}^k$  and k commitments  $[\mathsf{cm}_i]_{i=1}^k$ , the witness is  $([\vec{\mathbf{f}}_i]_{i=1}^k, \vec{\mathbf{w}})$  such that

- $(\mathbb{Z}_{ccs} := \sum_{i=1}^k b^{i-1} \cdot \mathbb{Z}_{w,i}, \vec{\mathbf{w}})$  is in the CCS relation,
- For every  $i \in [k]$ ,  $\vec{\mathbf{f}}_i$  is an opening of cm with norm less than b, and
- $\vec{\mathbf{w}} = \mathbf{G} \times \vec{\mathbf{f}}$  where  $\mathbf{G}$  is the gadget matrix and  $\vec{\mathbf{f}} := \sum_{i=1}^k b^{i-1} \vec{\mathbf{f}}_i$ .

We provide the formal definition below.

**Definition 4.4** (Splitted committed CCS relation). Let  $\mathcal{R}_q := \mathbb{Z}_q[X]/(X^d+1)$ , pp :=  $(\mathsf{pp}_{\mathsf{cm}}, \mathsf{pp}_{\mathsf{ccs}})$  be the parameters defined in <u>Definition 4.2</u> where  $\mathsf{pp}_{\mathsf{cm}} = (\kappa, m, B < q/2, \mathbf{A})$  and  $\mathsf{pp}_{\mathsf{ccs}} = (n_r, n_c, t, n_s, \mathsf{deg}, \ell_{\mathsf{in}}, \mathring{\mathfrak{s}})$ . Set  $\ell := m/n_c \in \mathbb{N}$  where  $B^{\ell} \geq q/2$ . Let  $\mathbf{G} := \mathbf{I}_{n_c} \otimes [1, B, \dots, B^{\ell-1}] \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n_c \times m}$  and set  $b, k \in \mathbb{N}$  such that  $b^k = B$ . The indexed relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k}$  is defined as

$$\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k} := \left\{ \begin{array}{c} \left(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{x} := [\mathsf{cm}_{i}, \mathsf{x}_{\mathsf{ccs},i}]_{i=1}^{k}; \mathsf{w} := (\mathsf{w}_{\mathsf{ccs}}, [\vec{\mathbf{f}}_{i}]_{i=1}^{k})\right) \ s.t. \\ \forall i \in [k] : (\mathsf{pp}_{\mathsf{cm}}, \mathsf{cm}_{i}; \vec{\mathbf{f}}_{i}) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{cm}}^{b} \land \\ \mathbf{z}_{\mathsf{ccs}} := (\mathsf{x}_{\mathsf{ccs}} := \sum_{i=1}^{k} b^{i-1} \cdot \mathsf{x}_{\mathsf{ccs},i}, 1, \mathsf{w}_{\mathsf{ccs}}) = \mathbf{G} \times (\sum_{i=1}^{k} b^{i-1} \cdot \vec{\mathbf{f}}_{i}) \\ \land (\mathsf{pp}_{\mathsf{ccs}}, \mathsf{x}_{\mathsf{ccs}}; \mathsf{w}_{\mathsf{ccs}}) \in \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{ccs}} \end{array} \right\}.$$
(39)

Set  $b,k\in\mathbb{N}$  such that  $b^k=B$ . It is clear that if  $([\mathbf{x}_{\mathbf{w},i},\mathsf{cm}_i]_{i=1}^k,([\vec{\mathbf{f}}_i]_{i=1}^k,\vec{\mathbf{w}}))$  is in the splitted committed CCS relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k}$ , then  $([\mathbf{x}_{\mathbf{w}},\mathsf{cm}],(\vec{\mathbf{f}},\vec{\mathbf{w}}))$  is in relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{cmccs}}^B$ , where

 $[\mathbf{z}_{\mathsf{ccs}}, \mathsf{cm}] := \sum_{i=1}^k b^{i-1} \cdot [\mathbf{z}_{\mathsf{w},i}, \mathsf{cm}_i] \text{ and } \vec{\mathbf{f}} := \sum_{i=1}^k b^{i-1} \vec{\mathbf{f}}_i.$  Hence, there is a straightforward reduction from  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{cmccs}}$  to  $\mathcal{R}^{b,k}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}$ . Therefore, to build a folding scheme for CCS, it suffices to set  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{comp}} := \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k}$  and  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{acc}} := \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^{B}$  and construct a reduction of knowledge from  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{comp}} \times \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{acc}}$  to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{acc}}$ . The construction consists of two steps.

Step 1: Decomposition. Run protocol  $\Pi_{\text{ccsdec}}^*$  (Figure 6), to reduce  $\mathcal{R}_{\text{acc}} := \mathcal{R}_{\text{evalccs}}^B$  to

$$(\mathcal{R}^b_{\mathsf{evalccs}})^k := \underbrace{\mathcal{R}^b_{\mathsf{evalccs}} \times \cdots \times \mathcal{R}^b_{\mathsf{evalccs}}}_{k}.$$

**Step 2: Batch Folding.** Reduce  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k} \times (\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^b)^k$  back to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^B$  by running the protocol  $\Pi_{\mathsf{batch}}$  below.

# <span id="page-48-0"></span>Batch Folding: The reduction from $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k} \times (\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^b)^k$ to $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^B$

Using the techniques from previous sections, we can perceive all of the following statements (underlying  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k}$  and  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^{b}$ ) as sumcheck statements:

- The multilinear evaluation statements underlying  $(\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^b)^k$ ;
- The linearized CCS statements (i.e.  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{lccs}}$  from Definition 4.3) underlying  $(\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^b)^k$ ;
- The range proof statements (with norm b) underlying  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k}$  and  $(\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^b)^k$ ;
- The high-degree CCS gate-check (i.e. Definition 4.1) underlying  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k}$ Intuitively, the protocol  $\Pi_{batch}$  runs a sumcheck protocol to reduce all statements above into a folded statement in  $\mathcal{R}_{\text{evalccs}}^B$ . We formally describe the protocol below. To make the

notation consistent with the folding protocol  $\Pi_{\mathsf{ccsfold}}$  (Figure 7), we denote  $\mathcal{L}$  and  $\mathcal{L}_w$  as the module homomorphisms  $\mathcal{L}(\vec{\mathbf{f}}) := \mathbf{A}\vec{\mathbf{f}}$  and  $\mathcal{L}_w(\vec{\mathbf{f}}) := \mathbf{G}\vec{\mathbf{f}}$  (where  $\mathbf{G}$  is the gadget matrix), and we set  $n_{in} := \ell_{in} + 1$  and  $n := n_c$ .

The protocol  $\Pi_{\mathsf{batch}}$  that reduces  $\mathcal{R}^{b,k}_{\mathsf{splitccs}} \times (\mathcal{R}^b_{\mathsf{evalccs}})^k$  to  $\mathcal{R}^B_{\mathsf{evalccs}}$ :

Parameters:  $c \in \mathbb{N}$ , strong sampling sets<sup>4</sup>  $\mathcal{C} := \mathbb{Z}_q \subseteq \mathcal{R}_q$  and  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$  with  $\|\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}\|_{\mathsf{op}} \leq c$ 

$$\bullet \ \mathbf{x} := \left( [\mathbf{x}_i := (\mathbf{\vec{r}}_i, \hat{\mathbf{v}}_i, y_i, [\mathbf{u}_i^j]_{j=1}^t, \mathbf{x}_{w,i})]_{i=1}^k, \mathbf{x}' := [\mathbf{x}_{\mathsf{ccs},i}, y_i']_{i=1}^k \right) \text{ and }$$

$$\bullet \ \mathbb{w} := \left( [\mathbb{w}_i \coloneqq (\vec{\mathbf{f}}_i, \vec{\mathbf{w}}_i)]_{i=1}^k, \mathbb{w}' \coloneqq (\mathbb{w}_{\mathsf{ccs}}, [\vec{\mathbf{f}}_i']_{i=1}^k) \right)$$

 $\textbf{Output:} \ \mathtt{x}_o := (\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o, \hat{\mathbf{v}}_o, y_o, [\mathbf{u}_o^j]_{j=1}^t, \mathtt{x}_{w,o}), \ \mathtt{w}_o := (\vec{\mathbf{f}}_o, \vec{\mathbf{w}}_o)$ 

The protocol  $\langle P(pp, x; w), V(pp, x) \rangle$ :

1.  $V \rightarrow P : V \text{ sends } P$ 

$$\vec{\beta} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{C}^{\log m}, \quad \gamma \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{C}, \quad [\alpha_i, \mu_i, \mu_i']_{i=1}^k \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} (\mathcal{C} \times \mathcal{C} \times \mathcal{C})^k, \quad [\zeta_i^j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{C}]_{i \in [k], j \in [t]}.$$

<span id="page-48-1"></span> $<sup>{}^{4}\</sup>mathcal{C}$  can be any strong sampling sets, we set  $\mathcal{C} := \mathbb{Z}_q$  for efficiency reasons.

2.  $V \leftrightarrow P : P$  and V run a sum-check protocol for the claim

$$\sum_{\vec{\mathbf{b}} \in \{0,1\}^{\log m}} g(\vec{\mathbf{b}}) = \sum_{i=1}^k \alpha_i \hat{\mathbf{v}}_i + \sum_{i=1}^k \sum_{j=1}^t \zeta_i^j \mathbf{u}_i^j.$$

Let  $d := \max(2b, \deg + 1)$ . The polynomial  $g(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) \in \mathcal{R}_q^{\leq d}[X_1, \dots, X_{\log m}]$  is defined as

$$g(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) := \gamma g_{\mathsf{ccs}}'(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) + \sum_{i=1}^{k} \left[ \alpha_i g_{\mathsf{eval}}^i(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) + \mu_i g_{\mathsf{rg}}^i(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) \right] + \sum_{i=1}^{k} \sum_{j=1}^{t} \zeta_i^j g_{\mathsf{lccs}}^{i,j}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) + \sum_{i=1}^{k} \mu_i' g_{\mathsf{rg}}^{'i}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) . \tag{40}$$

Set  $\mathbb{Z}_{ccs} := \sum_{i=1}^k b^{i-1} \mathbb{Z}_{ccs,i}$  and let  $\mathbf{z}_{ccs} := (\mathbb{Z}_{ccs}, 1, \mathbb{W}_{ccs})$ . The polynomial  $g'_{ccs}$  is

<span id="page-49-0"></span>
$$g'_{\mathsf{ccs}}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) := eq(\vec{\beta}, \vec{\mathbf{x}}) \cdot \left( \sum_{i=1}^{n_s} c_i \cdot \left[ \prod_{j \in S_i} \left( \sum_{\vec{\mathbf{y}} \in \{0,1\}^{\log n + n_{\mathsf{in}}}} \mathsf{mle}\left[M_j\right](\vec{\mathbf{x}}, \vec{\mathbf{y}}) \cdot \mathsf{mle}\left[\mathbf{z}_{\mathsf{ccs}}\right](\vec{\mathbf{y}}) \right] \right] \right). \tag{41}$$

For all  $i \in [k]$ ,

$$\begin{split} g_{\text{eval}}^i(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) &:= eq(\vec{\mathbf{r}}_i, \vec{\mathbf{x}}) \cdot \text{mle} \left[ \hat{\mathbf{f}}_i \right] \left( \vec{\mathbf{x}} \right), \\ g_{\text{rg}}^i(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) &:= eq(\vec{\beta}, \vec{\mathbf{x}}) \cdot \prod_{j=-(b-1)}^{b-1} \left( \text{mle} \left[ \hat{\mathbf{f}}_i \right] \left( \vec{\mathbf{x}} \right) - j \right), \\ g_{\text{rg}}^{'i}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) &:= eq(\vec{\beta}, \vec{\mathbf{x}}) \cdot \prod_{j=-(b-1)}^{b-1} \left( \text{mle} \left[ \hat{\mathbf{f}}_i^{\prime} \right] \left( \vec{\mathbf{x}} \right) - j \right). \end{split}$$

For all  $i \in [k], j \in [t]$ , denote  $\mathbf{z}_i := (\mathbf{z}_{w,i} || \vec{\mathbf{w}}_i) = \mathcal{L}_w(\vec{\mathbf{f}}_i)$ ,

$$g_{\mathsf{lccs}}^{i,j}(\vec{\mathbf{x}}) := \mathit{eq}(\vec{\mathbf{r}}_i, \vec{\mathbf{x}}) \cdot \left( \sum_{\vec{\mathbf{y}} \in \{0,1\}^{\log{(n+n_{\mathsf{in}})}}} \mathsf{mle}\left[M_j\right](\vec{\mathbf{x}}, \vec{\mathbf{y}}) \cdot \mathsf{mle}\left[\mathbf{z}_i\right](\vec{\mathbf{y}}) \right) \,.$$

The protocol reduces to check the evaluation claim  $g(\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o) \stackrel{?}{=} s$  where  $\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{C}^{\log m}$  is the sum-check challenge sampled by V.

3.  $P \rightarrow V : P \text{ sends } V \text{ values}$ 

$$\left[\theta_i := \mathsf{mle}\left[\hat{\mathbf{f}}_i\right](\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o); \, \theta_i' := \mathsf{mle}\left[\hat{\mathbf{f}}_i'\right](\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o)\right]_{i=1}^k;$$

For all  $i \in [k]$ ,  $j \in [t]$ , denote by  $\mathbf{z}_i := \mathcal{L}_w(\vec{\mathbf{f}}_i)$  and  $\mathbf{z}_i' := \mathcal{L}_w(\vec{\mathbf{f}}_i')$ . P send V

$$\eta^{i,j} := \sum_{\vec{\mathbf{v}} \in \{0,1\}^{\log{(n+n_{\mathrm{in}})}}} \mathsf{mle}\left[M_j\right](\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o,\vec{\mathbf{y}}) \cdot \mathsf{mle}\left[\mathbf{z}_i\right](\vec{\mathbf{y}})\,,$$

and

$$\eta_*^{i,j} := \sum_{\vec{\mathbf{y}} \in \{0,1\}^{\log{(n+n_{\mathrm{in}})}}} \mathsf{mle}\left[M_j\right](\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o, \vec{\mathbf{y}}) \cdot \mathsf{mle}\left[\mathbf{z}_i'\right](\vec{\mathbf{y}}) \,.$$

<span id="page-50-2"></span>4. V computes  $[\mathbf{e}_i := eq(\vec{\mathbf{r}}_i, \vec{\mathbf{r}}_o)]_{i=1}^k$  and  $\mathbf{e}^* := eq(\vec{\beta}, \vec{\mathbf{r}}_o)$  and checks that

$$s \stackrel{?}{=} \sum_{i=1}^{k} \left[ \alpha_{i} \mathbf{e}_{i} \theta_{i} + \mu_{i} \mathbf{e}^{*} \prod_{j=1-b}^{b-1} (\theta_{i} - j) \right] + \sum_{i=1}^{k} \sum_{j=1}^{t} \zeta_{i}^{j} \mathbf{e}_{i} \eta^{i,j}$$

$$+ \sum_{i=1}^{k} \mu'_{i} \mathbf{e}^{*} \prod_{j=1-b}^{b-1} (\theta'_{i} - j) + \gamma \mathbf{e}^{*} \left( \sum_{i=1}^{n_{s}} c_{i} \cdot \prod_{j \in S_{i}} \left( \sum_{\ell=1}^{k} \eta_{*}^{\ell,j} b^{\ell-1} \right) \right)$$

- 5.  $V \to P : V$  sends P random challenges  $[\rho_i]_{i=1}^k \stackrel{\leqslant}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}^k$  and  $[\rho_i']_{i=1}^k \stackrel{\leqslant}{\leftarrow} \mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}^k$
- <span id="page-50-1"></span>6. V output  $\mathbf{z}_o := (\vec{\mathbf{r}}_o, \hat{\mathbf{v}}_o, y_o, [\mathbf{u}_o^j]_{j=1}^t, \mathbf{z}_{w,o})$  where

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{NTT}(\hat{\mathbf{v}}_o) &= \sum_{i=1}^k \mathsf{RotSum}(\rho_i, \mathsf{NTT}(\theta_i)) + \sum_{i=1}^k \mathsf{RotSum}(\rho_i', \mathsf{NTT}(\theta_i')) \\ y_o &= \sum_{i=1}^k \rho_i \cdot y_i + \sum_{i=1}^k \rho_i' \cdot y_i' \\ \forall j \in [t] \, : \, u_o^j &= \sum_{i=1}^k \rho_i \eta^{i,j} + \sum_{i=1}^k \rho_i' \eta_*^{i,j} \\ \mathbf{x}_{w,o} &= \sum_{i=1}^k \rho_i \mathbf{x}_{w,i} + \rho_1' \cdot [\mathbf{x}_{\mathsf{ccs},1}, 1] + \sum_{i=2}^k \rho_i' \cdot [\mathbf{x}_{\mathsf{ccs},i}, 0] \,. \end{split}$$

7. P outputs  $\vec{\mathbf{f}}_o = \sum_{i=1}^k \rho_i \cdot \vec{\mathbf{f}}_i + \sum_{i=1}^k \rho'_i \cdot \vec{\mathbf{f}}'_i$  and  $\vec{\mathbf{w}}_o := \mathcal{L}_w(\vec{\mathbf{f}}_o)[n_{\mathsf{in}} + 1, n_{\mathsf{in}} + n]$ .

<span id="page-50-0"></span>Lemma 4.3. Let  $\mathcal{R}_q := \mathbb{Z}_q[X]/(X^d+1) \cong \mathbb{Z}_q^d$ ,  $\operatorname{pp} := (\operatorname{pp}_{\operatorname{cm}}, \operatorname{pp}_{\operatorname{ccs}})$  be the parameters defined in Definition 4.2 where  $\operatorname{pp}_{\operatorname{cm}} = (\kappa, m, B < q/2, \mathbf{A})$  and  $\operatorname{pp}_{\operatorname{ccs}} = (n_r = m, n_c, t, n_s, \deg, \ell_{\operatorname{in}}, \mathring{\mathbb{I}})$  such that  $B^{m/n_c} \geq q/2$ . Let  $\mathcal{C}$ ,  $\mathcal{C}_{\operatorname{small}}$  be strong sampling sets where  $1/|\mathcal{C}|, 1/|\mathcal{C}_{\operatorname{small}}| = \operatorname{negl}(\lambda)$  and  $\mathcal{C}_{\operatorname{small}}$  has expansion factor  $T := \|\mathcal{C}_{\operatorname{small}}\|_{\operatorname{op}} \leq c$  (Definition 6). Assume that  $\operatorname{MSIS}_{\kappa,m,8TB}^{\infty,q}$  is hard. For any b,k such that 2kc(b-1) < B and  $b^k = B$ ,  $\Pi_{\operatorname{batch}}$  is a reduction of knowledge from  $\mathcal{R}_{\operatorname{splitccs}}^{b,k} \times (\mathcal{R}_{\operatorname{evalccs}}^b)^k$  to  $\mathcal{R}_{\operatorname{evalccs}}^B$ .

*Proof.* We defer the proof to Appendix B.4.

By Lemma 4.3 and Lemma 4.2 and the knowledge composition theorem (Theorem 2.1), we obtain the theorem below.

<span id="page-51-1"></span>**Theorem 4.3.** Let  $\mathcal{R}_q$ ,  $\mathcal{C}$ ,  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$ ,  $\mathsf{pp}_{\mathsf{cm}} = (\kappa, m, B, \mathbf{A})$ ,  $\mathsf{pp}_{\mathsf{ccs}} = (n_r, n_c, t, n_s, \mathsf{deg}, \ell_{\mathsf{in}}, \mathring{\imath})$ , and b, k be defined as in Lemma 4.3.  $\Pi_{\mathsf{batch}} \circ \Pi_{\mathsf{ccsdec}}$  is a reduction of knowledge from  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k} \times \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^{B}$  to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{evalccs}}^{B}$ .

**Remark 4.5** (Supporting small prime modulus). The optimization in Section 3.3 can be used to extend Theorem 4.3 to support small modulus q, i.e., Theorem 4.3 still holds when  $\mathcal{R}_q \cong \mathbb{F}_{a^{\tau}}^{d/\tau}$  for any  $\tau \in \mathbb{N}$  that divides d.

IVC proof compression. In the final step of IVC/PCD, the final IVC verifier needs to check witnesses for two statements, one in  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{comp}}$  and one in  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{acc}}$ . A naive approach is letting the prover send the statement witnesses, and the verifier checks the statements in the clear. We can further improve the verifier complexity by generating another SNARK (e.g., Stark or LaBRADOR) that proves the correctness of the two final statements. Then the verifier only needs to check the SNARK proof.

However, in the optimized folding scheme for CCS, the statement in  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{comp}} := \mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{splitccs}}^{b,k}$  is more expensive to prove, as it involves checking the openings of k (rather than 1) commitments. Fortunately, we observe that in the last IVC step, there is no need to translate the online IVC statement into a *committed* CCS relation statement, because the *committed* CCS relation is only helpful when you need to *fold* the statement further. Instead, it is sufficient to translate the IVC statement as a CCS relation statement. The SNARK only needs to prove the IVC statement (plus the statement in the accumulated relation  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{acc}}$ ) without checking additional commitment openings inside the SNARK circuit.

Alternatively, one can also fold the last two statements, one in  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{comp}}$  and one in  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{acc}}$ , into a statement in  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{acc}}$ , so the SNARK only needs to prove a single statement in  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{acc}}$  (plus the folding verification logic). There is no need to prove any logic related to  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{comp}}$ . Additionally, we can make the final SNARK proof zero knowledge, thereby hiding secret information.

## <span id="page-51-0"></span>5 Performance Estimates

We specify the complexity of the folding schemes in Table 1. For CCS relations, we consider the optimized folding scheme in Section 4.3. Recall that  $\tau$  denote the extension field degree such that  $\mathcal{R}_q \cong \mathbb{F}_{q^\tau}$ ,  $\kappa$  is the rank of the MSIS matrix.  $(n_r, n_c, t, n_s, \deg, \ell_{\rm in})$  is the parameter where  $n_r$ ,  $n_c$  are the number of rows and columns of the CCS matrices, t is the number of matrices in the CCS relation,  $n_s$  is the number of sub-gates per CCS constraint, and  $\ell_{\rm in}$  is the public input length.

Each instance consists of  $\tau + \kappa + t + \ell_{\text{in}} + 1$   $\mathcal{R}_q$ -elements for the values  $[\hat{\mathbf{v}}_i]_{i=1}^{\tau}$ ,  $[\mathbf{u}_j]_{j=1}^{t}$ , commitment  $\mathsf{cm} \in \mathcal{R}_q^{\kappa}$  and public input  $\mathbf{z}_w$ ; additionally it takes  $\log m$  field elements for the challenge vector  $\vec{\mathbf{r}}$  in the strong sampling set  $\mathcal{C} \cong \mathbb{F}_{q^{\tau}}$ .

Recall that b, k are the parameters such that  $b^k$  equals the norm bound B. The prover takes  $O(mk(\kappa + t))$   $\mathcal{R}_q$  multiplications to compute the commitments and **u**-values for the

decomposed witness (Figure 6); and it takes  $O(mD\log^2 D)$   $\mathcal{R}_q$  multiplications to run the sum-check in protocol  $\Pi_{\mathsf{batch}}$  (Section 4.3.1), where  $D := \max(2b, \mathsf{deg})$ .

We split the verifier into three phases:

- It takes  $k \cdot (\kappa + \tau + t + \ell_{in} + 1) \mathcal{R}_q$  multiplications to check the correctness of decomposition in Figure 6;
- It takes  $n_s \deg + k(\tau + t + 4b\tau) \mathcal{R}_q$  multiplications to check the high-degree random evaluation claims and takes  $2D \log m \mathcal{R}_q$  multiplications<sup>5</sup> to run the sumcheck verifiers in Figure 5. Additionally, it takes  $2(k+1) \log m$  field multiplications to compute the k+1 of eq values.<sup>6</sup>
- It  $2k \cdot (\kappa + \tau + t + \ell_{\mathsf{in}} + 1) \mathcal{R}_q$  multiplications to fold the decomposed instances in  $\Pi_{\mathsf{batch}}$  (Section 4.3.1).
- Besides, the Fiat-Shamir transform takes  $\approx 2k\kappa$  hashes to absorb the inputs and the decomposed commitments, and it takes  $\approx 2k\log m$  hashes to compute the sumcheck verifier challenges.

| To highlight its practicality, we consider the following example ins | e instantiation. |
|----------------------------------------------------------------------|------------------|
|----------------------------------------------------------------------|------------------|

| Relation | Ajtai                                                                                          | CCS                                                                                                                                                                                 |
|----------|------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| Instance | $\begin{array}{ll} \mathcal{R}_q: & \tau + \kappa \ \mathbb{F}_{q^\tau}: & \log m \end{array}$ | $\mathcal{R}_q:  \tau + \kappa + t + \ell_{in} + 1$ $\mathbb{F}_{q^{\tau}}:  \log m$                                                                                                |
| Prover   | $\mathcal{R}_q$ -mul: $O(mk\kappa)+\ O(mb\log^2(b))$                                           | $\mathcal{R}_q$ -mul: $O(mk(\kappa+t))+ O(mD\log^2(D))$                                                                                                                             |
| Verifier | $\mathcal{R}_q\text{-mul}:  4k\cdot(\kappa+\tau) \  \  \  \  \  \  \  \  \  \  \  \  \ $       | $\mathcal{R}_q\text{-mul}:  3k\cdot(\kappa+\tau+t+\ell_{in}+1)+\\ n_s\deg+k(\tau+t+4b\tau)+\\ 2D\log m\\ \mathbb{F}\text{-mul}:  (2k+2)\log m\\ H: \qquad \approx 2k\kappa+D\log m$ |

<span id="page-52-0"></span>Table 1: The complexity of our folding schemes.  $\mathcal{R}_q \cong \mathbb{F}_{q^{\tau}}^{d/\tau}$  for some  $\tau, d \in \mathbb{N}$  where  $\tau \mid d$ .  $\kappa$  is the number of  $\mathcal{R}_q$ -elements in the Ajtai commitment;  $(t, n_s, \ell_{\text{in}}, \deg)$  are the CCS parameters in Definition 4.1. Let B be the norm bound of the committed witness and b, k are the parameters such that  $b^k = B$  and  $D := \max(2b, \deg)$ .  $\mathcal{R}_q$ -mul and  $\mathbb{F}$ -mul denote multiplications over  $\mathcal{R}_q \cong \mathbb{F}_{q^{\tau}}^{d/\tau}$  and  $\mathbb{F}_{q^{\tau}}$  respectively; H denotes a hash from  $\mathcal{R}_q^2$  to  $\mathcal{R}_q$ .

<span id="page-52-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>5</sup>Note that each univariate random evaluation takes  $\approx 2D$  multiplication in  $\mathcal{R}_q$ , where D is the degree of the univariate polynomial.

<span id="page-52-2"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>6</sup>The sum-check over  $\mathcal{R}_q$  can be understood as batching  $d/\tau$  sum-checks over field  $\mathbb{F}_{q^\tau}$ ; thus the verifier can simulate all sum-check operations over  $\mathbb{F}_{q^\tau}$  without any NTT inversions.

Instantiation. We set  $\kappa := 16$ , d := 64 and use a 64-bit prime q such that  $\mathcal{R}_q \cong \mathbb{F}_{q^4}^{d/4}$ . Assign the last challenge set  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$  as the set of elements in  $\mathcal{R}_q$  with coefficients -1, 0, 1 or 2. Since d = 64, we have that  $|\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}| = 4^{64} = 2^{128}$ . Moreover,  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$  is a strong sampling set, because the difference of any two distinct elements has infinite norm at most  $3 < \frac{q^{1/16}}{\sqrt{16}} = 4$  and thus is invertible by Lemma 2.3. By Lemma 2.2,  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$  has expansion factor at most 2d = 128. By the MSIS hardness bound in Section 2.2, we can achieve 128-bit security if

$$0.5 \cdot (\log m + \log d) + \log(8 \|\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}\|_{\mathsf{op}} \cdot B) \approx 2 \sqrt{\log_2(1.0045) d\kappa \log q}$$

which holds if  $\log B \le 29 - 0.5 \cdot \log m$ .

For simplicity, we set the CCS parameters  $(t, n_s, \ell_{\rm in})$  where  $\ell_{\rm in} = 0$ ,  $t = n_s = 1$ . Assume that the number of CCS constraints (over  $\mathcal{R}_q$ ) in the IVC/PCD recursive circuit is  $m \leq 2^{26}$ . We set  $B := 2^{16}$  so that  $\log B \leq 29 - 0.5 \cdot \log m \leq 16$ . We emphasize that by Remark 4.1, m constraints over  $\mathcal{R}_q$  can be used to pack md/4 = 16m constraints over  $\mathbb{F}_{q^4}$ , so an upper bound  $m \leq 2^{26}$  leads to an upper bound  $2^{30}$  on the number of constraints over  $\mathbb{F}_{q^4}$ , which is more than enough.

Let's see the concrete instance size, prover cost and verifier circuit size. We set b := 2 and k := 16 so that  $b^k = 2^{16} = B$  and  $2kc(b-1) = 2 \cdot 16 \cdot 128 \cdot 1 < B = 2^{16}$  as required in Theorem 4.2.

The instance size is  $\tau + \kappa + t + \ell_{\text{in}} + 1 = 22 \mathcal{R}_q$ -elements and  $\log m \mathbb{F}_{q^{\tau}}$ -elements. E.g., for  $m := 2^{26}$ , this is  $\approx 12 \text{KB}$ . The recursive folding verifier takes

$$|V| \approx (1648 + (2D + 2) \cdot \log m) \cdot |\mathcal{R}_q| + (2k\kappa + D\log m) \cdot |H|$$

CCS constraints. Here  $|\mathcal{R}_q|$  denotes the number of constraints for a single  $\mathcal{R}_q$  multiplication,  $|\mathsf{H}|$  denotes the number of constraints for simulating a two-to-one hash. Note that  $|\mathcal{R}_q| \leq 1$  as it takes at most one contraint to simulate an  $\mathcal{R}_q$  multiplication; by [Bou+23], we can set  $|\mathsf{H}| \leq 100$ . The number of constraints is dominated by the Fiat-Shamir circuit, which takes  $\approx 50 \mathrm{K}$  constraints.

<span id="page-53-0"></span>**Remark 5.1.** After fixing the parameter b, instead of setting k such that  $b^k = B$ , we can set  $k^*$  as the minimal integer such that  $b^{k^*} \geq 2k^*(b-1)\|\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}\|_{\mathsf{op}}$  where  $\|\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}\|_{\mathsf{op}}$  is the expansion factor of  $\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}$ . In each folding step, we decompose the folded witness to  $k^* \leq k$  parts, leading to an efficiency improvement. This works because after each folding step, the norm of the folded witness is always less than  $2k^*(b-1)\|\mathcal{C}_{\mathsf{small}}\|_{\mathsf{op}} \leq b^{k^*}$ . This also indicates that we can use different decomposition factors  $k^*$ , k for relations  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{acc}}$  and  $\mathcal{R}_{\mathsf{comp}}$ .

**Remark 5.2** (Supporting non-power-of-two cyclotomic rings). Our current analysis requires  $\mathcal{R} := \mathbb{Z}[X]/(X^d+1)$  to be a cyclotomic ring where d is a power-of-two. We conjecture that the scheme remains secure when using a non-power-of-two cyclotomic ring and leave the formal analysis for future work.

## <span id="page-54-0"></span>6 Discussion of an Alternative Approach

In this section we discuss another technique for proving a norm bound on a committed vector, called *random projection*. We explain why this approach, which may at first seem appealing, does not seem to work in the context of folding.

Suppose we aim to fold 2k instance-witness pairs  $[\varkappa_i, w_i]_{i=1}^{2k}$  of an Ajtai commitment-based relation (e.g.,  $\mathcal{R}_{\text{eval}}^b$  from Eq. (9)) to a single instance-witness pair  $(\varkappa_o, w_o)$  in a related relation (e.g.,  $\mathcal{R}_{\text{eval}}^b$  where B > b). To extract knowledge of  $[w_i]_{i=1}^{2k}$  (with  $||w_i||_{\infty} < b$  for all  $i \in [2k]$ ) from a folding prover P\* that outputs correct witness in  $\mathcal{R}_{\text{eval}}^B$ , the most naive approach is to have the prover directly transmit  $[w_i]_{i=1}^{2k}$  and let the verifier check that  $[w_i]_{i=1}^{2k}$  have small norms and are consistent with the folded instance. This certainly does not work as the verifier has complexity linear to the witness size while constructing a IVC/PCD requires folding verifiers to be sublinear. Alternatively, the prover could generate a proof demonstrating that each element of  $[w_i]_{i=1}^{2k}$  has a small norm, but this method is excessively costly due to the requirement for  $\Theta(m)$  range-check circuits, where m is the witness length.

To circumvent these challenges, a natural idea is to leverage the random projection technique from LaBRADOR [BS23]: The verifier samples and sends a random matrix  $\Pi \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{\lambda d \times md}$  with small norms, where  $\lambda$  is the security parameter and m is the size of  $\vec{\mathbf{v}} := [\mathbf{w}_i]_{i=1}^{2k}$ . Subsequently, the prover sends  $\vec{\mathbf{v}} := \Pi \vec{\mathbf{w}} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{\lambda d}$  and the verifier checks that  $\|\vec{\mathbf{v}}\|_{\infty}$  is small and  $\vec{\mathbf{v}}$  is computed honestly. Notably, the size of  $\vec{\mathbf{v}}$  is independent of the witness size. Additionally, if  $\|\vec{\mathbf{v}}\|_{\infty}$  has a small norm, by the Johnson-Lindenstrauss Lemma [WL84; GHL22; BS23], the original witnesses also have small norms with high probability (over the random choice of  $\Pi$ ). Nonetheless, several challenges arise in the context of folding schemes.

First, the size of the matrix  $\Pi$  is large, making it impractical for the verifier to generate  $\Pi$  itself. A potential solution involves having the verifier generate a short random seed s, which the prover then uses to generate  $\Pi$  and subsequently proves the correctness of  $\Pi$ 's generation. However, this approach introduces significant complexity in terms of circuit size, as simulating PRG computations in circuits for a large output is prohibitively expensive.

Second, how does the verifier check that  $\vec{\mathbf{v}}$  was computed honestly? It's impractical for the verifier to directly receive  $\vec{\mathbf{w}}$  and verify its correctness, as this would result in a linear-sized verifier. An alternative approach could be to have the prover generate another instance-witness pair  $(\mathbf{z}', \mathbf{w}')$  for proving  $\vec{\mathbf{v}} = \Pi \vec{\mathbf{w}}$  and then fold it together with the original instances. However, this leads to a chicken-and-egg problem: how do we check that the committed witness in  $\mathbf{w}'$  has a small norm? The most viable solution appears to involve the prover computing a post-quantum secure SNARK  $\pi$  for proving that  $\vec{\mathbf{v}} = \Pi \vec{\mathbf{w}}$  and  $\Pi = \mathsf{PRG}(s)$  (where s is the short random seed), with the verifier subsequently verifying the correctness of  $\pi$ . Concretely, this solution is inefficient due to the high complexity of the SNARK verifier circuit. Furthermore, since we can already construct IVC/PCD directly

from SNARKs [\[BCCT13;](#page-59-7) [BCTV14\]](#page-59-0), employing folding in conjunction with SNARKs serves little purpose.

Even if we manage to overcome the aforementioned challenges, we still encounter an inherent obstacle: the random projection idea cannot guarantee perfect completeness: Even if the prover is honest and provides input witnesses with genuinely small norms, there remains a small probability that the random projection ⃗v = Πw⃗ would yield large norms, resulting in rejection by the verifier. We note that perfect completeness is essential for constructing an IVC/PCD [\[B¨un+21\]](#page-61-2), and it appears inherently difficult to construct a folding scheme that achieves perfect completeness using the random projection approach.

## <span id="page-55-0"></span>7 Conclusion, open problems, and future work

We presented LatticeFold, the first lattice folding scheme based on the Module SIS problem. Our folding protocol ensures that the witnesses extracted from a folded statement always satisfy the required norm bounds. This is done by requiring the prover to prove that its starting witnesses are all low norm. This proof is done efficiently using the sumcheck protocol.

There are many directions for future work. First, it is not difficult to extend the scheme to support the Lasso [\[STW23b;](#page-65-6) [ST23\]](#page-64-10) lookup argument. This is because the sumcheck used by Lasso is compatible with the sumchecks in LatticeFold. Second, it remains to implement LatticeFold and experiment with its real-world performance. We estimate that LatticeFold is competitive with the best pre-quantum folding schemes. It is likely to be the most performant folding system for computations using high-degree CCS. LatticeFold could be an example where (plausible) post-quantum security leads to better performance.

Finally, it would be interesting to explore the performance of LatticeFold using other lattice-based additively homomorphic commitments schemes, for example, ones based on SIS rather MSIS. In addition, recall that much of the work in the design of LatticeFold is due to the fact that the Ajtai commitment scheme [\[Ajt96\]](#page-57-0) is binding only when the committed vector is low norm. If we had a lattice-based linearly homomorphic commitment that was binding for arbitrary vectors, irrespective of their norm, then one could more directly use that commitment scheme in the Hypernova [\[KS23b\]](#page-63-2) or Protostar [\[BC23\]](#page-58-1) systems. Such commitment schemes exist (e.g., [\[Bau+18b\]](#page-58-7)), however they are not succinct: the commitment string is quite long. Using them would result in a SNARK with poor prover performance and long proofs. Designing a succinct lattice-based linearly homomorphic commitment scheme for committing to vectors of arbitrary norm is an interesting area for further research.

Acknowledgments. We thank the anonymous CRYPTO reviewers and Wilson Nguyen for their valuable feedback, and Srinath Setty for bringing up the question of batching all Sumchecks. Opinions, findings, and conclusions or recommendations expressed in this material are those of the authors and do not necessarily reflect the views of DARPA.
